Unit 1 Health Matters

Lesson 1: Accidents

Vocabulary Tip 1: Word sets
- A good way to learn new words is to put connected words in sets:
Fruit: apples oranges grapes bananas strawberries

A Study the Vocabulary Tip, then, using a dictionary, check the meaning of any words you donâ€™t know. Write the words below in the correct set. There are five words in each set.

ankle bandage blood bones breathe cough cream elbow faint heart knee medicine plaster pills shoulder skin sneeze stomach swallow wrist

Set Words

Treatment: bandage

Joints: ankle

Verbs: breathe

Inside and outside the body: blood bones

B Work in pairs. Student A chooses one of the problems a to n on pages 8 and 9 of the Course Book, but doesnâ€™t tell Student B. Student A mimes the problem and Student B guesses whatâ€™s wrong and gives advice.

Example: Student A licks his lips and touches them.
- Whatâ€™s the matter? Are your lips sore?
- Yes. And theyâ€™re really dry.
- You shouldnâ€™t touch them. It will make them worse. You should put cream on them.

Vocabulary Tip 2: Word groups

â€¢ Words with similar meanings are not always interchangeable. It is easier to use a word correctly if you know whether it is a verb, a noun or an adjective. Be careful with these words: hurt, sore, pain.
â€¢ Hurt is a verb. The past simple is also hurt.
  My back hurts today. It also hurt yesterday. My feet hurt today. They hurt yesterday as well.
â€¢ Sore is an adjective.
  My back is sore. My feet are also sore. They weren't sore yesterday.
â€¢ Pain is a noun. In the singular it usually has an article.
  I have a pain in my stomach. Have you taken any medication for the pain?
  Yes, I took some painkillers an hour ago. Do you have pains anywhere else?

C Complete each sentence with a word from the box.
bleeding   broken   hurts   pain   sick   sore   dizzy   sneeze

1 'Have you got a cold?' 'No, I always ____________ when I put pepper on my food.'
2 He was ____________ three times in the night. I think the food at the restaurant where he ate wasn't very fresh.
3 She cut her hand while she was chopping vegetables. It was a very deep cut and it was ____________ a lot, so she had to go to hospital and have it stitched.
4 My back ____________ all the time. It only feels OK when I am lying down.
5 She can't play tennis. She has ____________ her right arm.
6 I went swimming yesterday and now my eyes are ____________ from the chemicals in the pool.
7 Where exactly is the ____________ and how long have you had it?
8 It was a very hot day and she hadn't eaten or drunk anything all day. That's why she suddenly went pale and felt ____________ during the lesson.

Unit 1

Lesson 2: At the Hospital

Describing instances in the past: Using while, as and when to talk about past actions

- We can use the past continuous and the past simple in the same sentence to show that one action happened in the middle of another action. We use the past continuous for the longer action and the past simple for the shorter action.
  I was riding my bicycle and I fell over.

- We often use while and as with the past continuous tense.
  While I was eating breakfast, a bird flew into the kitchen.
  As I was eating breakfast, a bird flew into the kitchen.

- We often use when or and with the past simple tense.
  I was eating breakfast when a bird flew into the kitchen.
  I was eating breakfast and a bird flew into the kitchen.

  CB p.90

Practise the dialogue in pairs. Then practise it again, changing the words in italics.

Adel: I've hurt my wrist.
Nurse: Sit down, please. I need to take your details.
Nurse: The doctor will see you very soon, Adel.
Doctor: How did you do it?
Adel: I was ice skating and I fell over.
Adel: Do you think itâ€™s broken?
Doctor: Probably not. But you need to have an X-ray to make sure.
Adel: Is it broken?
Doctor: Iâ€™m afraid so. Itâ€™s fractured here.
Doctor: Iâ€™m going to put your wrist in plaster.
Adel: How long will I have to keep it on?
Doctor: Six weeks.

Vocabulary Tip 1: Adjectives ending -ing or -ed
â€¢ Don't forget the difference between frightening and frightened, boring and bored, interesting and interested.
The -ed words describe the person who has the feeling.
The -ing words describe what or who gives them that feeling.

E
Underline the correct word.

1. She is not very interested / interesting in fashion. She prefers books and history.

2. I saw a very excited / exciting film on TV last night.

3. This book is very bored / boring. I fall asleep whenever I try to read it.

4. We were very frightened / frightening when our car broke down in the desert.

5. Heâ€™s a very interested / interesting person. He has lots of great stories to tell.

â€¢ Remember there is only one l in adjectives ending ~ful,

â€¢ But the adverb ending is ~fully,
e.g., beautifully, peacefully.

Vocabulary Tip 1: Phrasal verbs

â€¢ With some phrasal verbs, noun objects can come before or after the preposition.
  Turn off the light.
  OR: Turn the light off.
â€¢ With phrasal verbs, pronoun objects must come before the preposition.
  Turn it off.
  NOT: Turn off it.

Unit 1

Vocabulary Tip 2: Prefixes meaning not

â€¢ To form the opposite of some adjectives we can put the prefix un- in front of them, e.g., unhappy, unpopular, unintelligent.
â€¢ The prefixes in-, im-, il- or ir- can also form the opposite, e.g., inexpensive, impossible, illegal, irregular.
â€¢ The prefix un- can go in front of any letter. The prefix im- can only go in front of m or p, e.g., unhelpful, unrealistic, improper, impolite.
â€¢ The prefix il- can only go in front of l. The prefix ir- can only go in front of r, e.g. illiterate, irreplaceable.

  a few people; a few ______; too many people; not many ______

Using the correct tense: Timelines and past events

â€¢ One way of helping you remember the right tense is to use timelines. 
  a past habits/states which are now finished
    I used to live in a big house.
  
  b action that happened at a specific point in the past
    Last year I moved to a smaller house.
  
  c one action that happened while another action was happening in the past
    While I was moving, I found some old photos.

Reading Tip: Identifying the main idea

The topic sentence usually contains the main idea of the paragraph.

B Study the Reading Tip then read the first paragraph of the text more carefully and look at the question in 1.

1 What is the main idea of the first paragraph?
   a The Air Force is more fun because you can pilot planes.
   b You can do many different things in the military.
   c You have to be 18 years old to join the military.

Look at choice a). The problem with this answer is that it doesnâ€™t give information that is in the first paragraph. Some people may think that piloting a plane is fun, but this is not what the text says. It simply tells you that if you join the Air Force, you can work with aeroplanes and helicopters. So choice a) is incorrect.

Choice c) does repeat information that is found in the first paragraph, since the last sentence of the paragraph is You must be 18 years old to join. But if you look at the other sentences, you will see that they mostly deal with the different activities people can do in the military. The last sentence gives additional information that is not directly related to the activities that people can do in the military. So choice c) is incorrect.

Choice b) is the correct choice, since most of the sentences in the paragraph tell you about the variety of military jobs.

Unit 2

Lesson 6: A Robbery

Reading Tip: Skimming and scanning
â€¢ When you are looking for information in a text, you can save a lot of time if you use the right technique.
Two common techniques are skimming and scanning. Skimming means looking at the whole text quickly, without reading it in detail, to understand what the text is about. Scanning is letting your eyes go over the text quickly to search for specific information. Scanning is a good technique when you know what you are looking for.

A Scan the texts on page 26 of the Course Book, and circle the correct answer.

1 Who called the police?
   a Sue
   b Mr Smith
   c Mrs Smith

2 What woke Sue up?
   a the police
   b the telephone
   c a loud noise

3 Who saw the robber?
   a a witness
   b the police
   c Sue

B Read the texts more carefully and answer the questions.

1 Why are the police reminding homeowners to lock their doors and windows?

2 Why is Sue happy that the robber left before her father went downstairs?

3 How does Sue feel now?

4 Why is Sue upset that her father's computer was stolen?

5 Why is Sueâ€™s father getting the window lock replaced?

Verbs for actions done or caused by someone/something else: have, get and make
â€¢ Use have and get + object + past participle when something is done to or for someone.
  My father had his computer stolen. (Someone stole my dadâ€™s computer.)
  My father is getting the lock replaced today. (Someone is replacing the lock.)

â€¢ Use make + object + infinitive when someone or something causes something to happen.
  My father made me go back to my room.
  The teacher made us read three articles for our project.

CB p.91

Expressing conditional situations in the future: Prolonged conditional actions in the future

- We usually have the present simple in the if clause. But we can sometimes use the present continuous for actions that are prolonged.
  If itâ€™s raining tomorrow, we wonâ€™t go for a picnic.
  If theyâ€™re still working, we can wait until theyâ€™ve finished.

- In the main clause, we can use the future simple with will/wonâ€™t, the present continuous with future reference, going to, can, might, should or an imperative.
  If you pass, what will you do next year?
  If he gets a visa, heâ€™s leaving next week.
  If you do that again, Iâ€™m going to get really annoyed.
  If you get here early, we can work on our projects together.
  If it isnâ€™t too hot tomorrow, I might play tennis.
  If he asks you for money, you shouldnâ€™t give him any.
  If he asks you for money, donâ€™t give him any.

Retelling information: Reported questions â€“ tense changes

Direct question
â€˜Do you know?â€™ I asked him.
â€˜Are you OK?â€™ he asked me.
â€˜What are you doing?â€™ he asked me.
â€˜Did you have fun?â€™ she asked.
â€˜Where has she been?â€™ I asked.
â€˜What will they say?â€™ she asked.
â€˜Can you help?â€™ he asked me.

Reported question
I asked him if he __________ knew ______________.
He asked me if I _________________ OK.
He asked me what I _________________ doing.
She asked if I had _________________ fun.
I asked where she _________________ been.
She asked what they _________________ say.
He asked me whether I _________________ help.

â€¢ In reported questions we do not use the question form of the verb.

â€¢ We use if or whether for reported yes/no questions.

Lesson 5: Expressing Regret

A Study the rules and complete the examples.

Expressing regret: Using If only and I wish

I wish you had told me about your problem. If only you had told me about your problem. (= Then I could have helped).

I wish I ___________ gone to bed so late. If only I had gone to bed early. (= Then I wouldnâ€™t have been so tired.)

I wish I ___________ more carefully. If only I had driven more carefully. (= Then I wouldnâ€™t have crashed the car.)

â€¢ We use If only or I wish + past perfect to express regret about the past.

C Play the had to chain game in teams of six or more students. First, give the whole team a job, profession or business. Then each student in the team has to say what they had to do in their job last week. The next student has to repeat what the previous student said, and add something.

Example: Owner of a restaurant

Student A: Last week, I had to go to the market with the chef.
Student B: Last week, I had to go to the market with the chef, and I had to order a new fridge.
Student C: Last week, I had to go to the market with the chef, I had to order a new fridge, and I had to interview two waiters.

Vocabulary Tip: Compound nouns

- Compound nouns can be found in three different forms:
  â€¢ as in most of this exercise, where they are two separate nouns (or adjective + noun), e.g. footstep, bookshop.
  â€¢ those that are linked by a hyphen, e.g. horse-riding, singer-songwriter.
  â€¢ those that have become one word, e.g. armchair, teacup.

There is no clear rule about which form to use, and the form changes over time. When words are often used together, they might become hyphenated and later become one word.
Examples of two words becoming one are much more common than hyphenated words. For example, cardboard, footstep, lifestyle, sunset.

â€¢ We use ever to ask about possibility in the past,

â€¢ We use never to express the negative about past experiences,

Talking about completed or expected actions: Using just, already and yet

â€¢ We use just + present perfect when we talk about something that happened a short time ago,
e.g., You look nice. Have you just been to the hairdresser?

â€¢ We use already + present perfect when we talk about something that happened before we expected it,
e.g., Itâ€™s only eight oâ€™clock but Samira has already gone to bed.

â€¢ We use yet + present perfect in negative sentences and questions when we talk about something we expect to happen,
e.g., I havenâ€™t done my exams yet. [But I will soon.]
Have you passed your driving test yet? [I expect you have, or will soon.]

Talking about present actions that started in the past: Present perfect continuous

â€¢ We use the present perfect continuous to ask and answer about activities that began in the past and have just finished or are still going on now.

Sequencing of events in the past: Had + past perfect

â€¢ We form the past perfect with had + the past participle of the main verb.
â€¢ We use the past perfect for two events in the past when there is a need to show which came first.
â€¢ Match each statement with the statement that best describes it.

â˜ We couldnâ€™t get a room because we hadnâ€™t booked.
â˜ When I saw my brother again, his hair had gone grey.
â˜ After I had done my homework, I went to the cinema.
â˜ I arrived at the hotel at 12 and went straight to my room.

A: Talking about change.
B: Talking about two events but making it clear what happened first.
C: Talking about a simple series of events in the past, without using the past perfect.
D: Talking about two events when the first event is an explanation of what happened next, often using because or so.

Lesson 7: A Medical Tourist Destination

A Read the Functional Grammar box and think of four more examples.

Relative clauses: Giving more information

Defining relative clauses
Read the sentences and pay attention to the words in bold. Then match each sentence to the word that best describes what it is about. You will use one letter twice.
a ownership b people c places d things

The man who lives next door has just bought a private jet.
The bike which I borrowed yesterday is blue.
What have you done with the book that was on the table?
The laboratory where I work has very powerful computers.
The girl whose microscope I borrowed yesterday is called Layla.

Do the sentences give essential information or non-essential information?

Non-defining relative clauses
Non-defining relative clauses give a non-essential piece of information.
We use who for people, which for things and where for places. We cannot use that. We use whose to show possession.
When combining sentences to make a relative clause, there is incorporated into where, e.g., The University of ... is excellent. I studied English there. The University of ..., where I studied English, is excellent.

There is a comma (,) at the beginning of a non-defining relative clause, and a comma or a full stop at the end of it.

My father, who is interested in astronomy, buys two or three new books a month.
I lent the book to Saeed, who loves reading.
Misrata, which is about 200 kilometres east of Tripoli, is a popular tourist site.
The Galapagos, where I spent my last holiday, is famous for its wildlife conservation.
My brother, whose car was in an accident yesterday, is in hospital.

Writing Tip: Brainstorming ideas
It is sometimes difficult to think of ideas for an essay, particularly in an exam. One answer is to write down anything you can think of connected with the title, even unimportant things. This is called â€˜brainstormingâ€™. When you read the things you have written down, you will probably find some that you can use. Thinking of ideas quickly is really important, especially in an exam.

Focusing on the action: Passive forms

â€¢ We use the passive form when the focus of the sentence is on the action.
This money was deposited last week.

â€¢ We can use by if we want to say who did the action.
The money was deposited last week by my uncle.

â€¢ The passive is often used to describe a process. However, it should not be overused.
When cash is withdrawn from an ATM, it is taken from your account.
The transaction is shown on your statement.

â€¢ To change from active to passive, we use to be + past participle. Note the tense change in the examples:
They are renovating the bank.
The bank is being renovated.
They sent the bank statement yesterday.
The bank statement was sent yesterday.
Your statement will show the withdrawal.
The cash withdrawal will be shown on your statement.
They have made three deposits into my account since last week.
Three deposits have been made into my account since last week.

ğŸ”— CB p.95

D Unscramble the words to make passive sentences.

1 Somebody stole my wallet last week. (wallet week was my last stolen)
   
2 Somebody robbed the bank yesterday. (was the yesterday bank robbed)
   
3 Somebody will pay the bill tomorrow. (tomorrow bill will the be paid)

B Find conditional sentences in the conversations. Read the Functional Grammar box and identify what type they are.

Talking about real or imaginary conditions and results: Types of conditional sentences

â€¢ There are four basic types of conditional sentences: zero, first, second and third.
We use:

| Conditional | Usage | Example |
|-------------|-------|---------|
| Zero        | To talk about something that is always true. | If you look at a computer screen for too long, your eyes get tired. |
| First       | To talk about the result of a possible action in the future. | If you send the parcel today, it will arrive on Thursday. |
| Second      | To talk about an action that depends on an imaginary present or future situation or action. | If I had a lot of money, I would buy a big boat. |
| Third       | When we talk about a desire to change something in the past. This is impossible as the actions are finished. | If I'd taken a taxi, I would have arrived on time. |

Note: Sometimes we can use could instead of would in conditional sentences.
â€¢ Conditional sentences have two parts: the if clause and the main conditional clause.
The order is not usually important, but when the if clause comes first, they are separated by a comma:
If I hadnâ€™t been in such a hurry, I wouldnâ€™t have left it there!
I wouldnâ€™t have left it there if I hadnâ€™t been in such a hurry!

Writing Tip: Formal and informal letters
â€¢ We write formal letters/emails to people who we donâ€™t know very well.
These are often official letters/emails where we can ask for information, make complaints, apply for jobs, etc. In formal letters/emails, we donâ€™t use contractions, idioms or colloquial expressions. There are also many standard expressions. In formal letters/emails, passive forms are quite common.

â€¢ We write informal letters/emails to friends and family â€“ people we know well.
These letters/emails often use language which is similar to spoken language. We use contractions, idioms and colloquial expressions. The language is generally more friendly.

Unit 7

Read the Study Tip about making inferences.

Reading Tip: Making inferences

As discussed in Unit 5, you often have to make inferences to answer questions. Making inferences involves understanding information that is not clearly stated in the text. You decide what makes sense based on the information you have in the text.

Although this may sound difficult, you do it all the time without realizing it. For example, if you read a sentence that says: Ahmed put his swimsuit and suncream in his bag, you can infer that Ahmed is going to the beach or outdoor pool.

What can you infer from the information in Paragraph 1?
a) A lot of people are looking for jobs or want to get a better job.
b) Many people are learning about computers for their pleasure.

It may be true that many people are learning about computers for their pleasure, but we donâ€™t have any reason to infer this from the information in Paragraph 1, so the answer is not b).

But the sentence: Learning new computer skills can also help you change careers, and the number of people enrolling in computer classes is increasing, tells us that learning about computers can help you get a new job, and also that more and more people are taking computer classes. We can infer that these people want to get better jobs, so the answer is a).

Choose a or b to say which inference you can make from the article on page 77 of the Course Book. Underline the sentence or sentences that helped you decide.

Paragraph 2
a If you are a banker, you should learn to use spreadsheets.
b More and more teachers are learning to use spreadsheets.

Paragraph 2
a Web design is easy.
b More and more companies have websites.

Paragraph 3
a Students donâ€™t have to take computer classes.
b Many universities offer classes for people who work.

Unit 7

Lesson 5: Volunteers at the Hospital

A Read the paragraphs on page 79 of the Course Book. Make notes about the questions you discussed.

B Read about linking ideas in a paragraph.

Writing tip: Linking ideas

The ideas in a paragraph can be linked in different ways. Understanding how the ideas are connected can help you understand what the author is saying. Some of the most common types of links are:

A Going from general to specific information
The author makes a statement and gives examples or details. Some common linking words are: for example, for instance, such as.

B Cause and effect
The author explains that an event took place and what the result was. Some common linking words are: as a result, therefore, so.

C Time order
The author talks about what happened first, next, last. Some common linking words are: first, next, then, after that, once, as soon as.

D Comparison or contrast
The author talks about how things are the same or different. Some common linking words are: however, whereas, like, unlike, while.

Lesson 7: Books and the Internet

A Read about opinion essays.

Writing Tip: Opinion essays
In an essay expressing opinion, you say what you think about a subject. But it isn't enough to say what you think, you also need to say why you think this. This makes your essay more convincing and interesting.

The essay begins with an introduction, and this usually mentions what you are going to write about in the main part of your essay.

The body of the essay gives more details and the reasons for your opinion. If there are two sides to a question, you can write about one side in one paragraph, and the other in the next paragraph.

In the conclusion you can sum up your ideas and your opinion.

Vocabulary Tip: Understanding new words â€“ prefixes and suffixes

Learning prefixes and suffixes can help you understand and remember new words.
â€¢ The prefix de- means to remove, reduce or undo.
â€¢ The suffix ~ation means an action or process.
So the word deforestation means the action of removing or reducing a forest.
